Now, we need to present the proof.

Let's write a rigorous solution.

I will start by defining f(N) and deriving properties.

**Step 1: Characterization of f(N).** For N with at least three proper divisors, let d_1 < d_2 < d_3 be the three smallest divisors of N greater than 1. Then the three largest proper divisors are N/d_1, N/d_2, N/d_3, so f(N) = N/d_1 + N/d_2 + N/d_3. Note that d_1, d_2, d_3 are divisors of N.

**Step 2: Fixed points.** If f(N) = N, then 1/d_1 + 1/d_2 + 1/d_3 = 1. The only solution with distinct positive integers is (2,3,6). Thus, N must have 2,3,6 as its three smallest divisors >1. Hence, 2|N, 3|N, and no divisor between 3 and 6 (so N is not divisible by 4 or 5). So N = 6k with k odd and 5∤k. Conversely, any such N is a fixed point. (We can check that for N=6k, with k odd and 5∤k, the divisors are 2,3,6,... and 4 and 5 are not divisors, so d_1=2,d_2=3,d_3=6, thus f(N)=N.)

**Step 3: Iteration analysis.** For any a_1 with at least three proper divisors, define the sequence. We need to determine when all terms have at least three proper divisors.

First, note that if a_1 is odd, then it is composite and not a prime square. Then its smallest prime factor p ≥ 3. Since it has at least three proper divisors, p is the smallest divisor >1. The next smallest divisor d_2 is either p^2 or the next prime q. In any case, d_2 ≥ 5. Also, d_3 ≥ 7. Thus, f(a_1) = a_1/d_1 + a_1/d_2 + a_1/d_3 ≤ a_1/3 + a_1/5 + a_1/7 < a_1. So a_2 is odd and strictly less than a_1. Since a_1 is finite, the sequence of odd numbers is strictly decreasing, so eventually it must reach a term that has fewer than three proper divisors. **Solution:**

Let $f(N)$ denote the sum of the three largest proper divisors of $N$ (when $N$ has at least three proper divisors). For a sequence $a_1,a_2,\ldots$ defined by $a_{n+1}=f(a_n)$, we require every $a_n$ to have at least three proper divisors.

**1. Fixed points.** If $f(N)=N$, then the three smallest divisors $>1$ of $N$ satisfy $1/d_1+1/d_2+1/d_3=1$. The only solution in distinct positive integers is $(d_1,d_2,d_3)=(2,3,6)$. Hence $2\mid N$, $3\mid N$, and no divisor $d$ with $3<d<6$ exists, i.e., $4\nmid N$ and $5\nmid N$. Thus $N$ is a multiple of $6$ but not of $4$ or $5$, so $N=6k$ with $k$ odd and $5\nmid k$. Conversely, any such $N$ is a fixed point.

**2. Numbers that are not multiples of $6$ lead to failure.** If $a_1$ is odd, its smallest prime factor $p\ge3$; then its three smallest divisors $>1$ are at least $3,5,7$, so $f(a_1)\le a_1/3+a_1/5+a_1/7<a_1$. The sequence of odd numbers is strictly decreasing and bounded below by $1$, so it must eventually reach a number with fewer than three proper divisors (a prime or a prime square). Hence $a_1$ cannot be odd. If $a_1$ is even but not a multiple of $3$, write $a_1=2^a t$, $t$ odd, $3\nmid t$. Then the three smallest divisors are $2$, $4$ (if $a\ge2$) or the smallest odd prime $q\ge5$ (if $a=1$), and a third divisor $d_3\ge5$ (or $2q$). In either case, one can check that $f(a_1)<a_1$ (the largest possible $f(a_1)/a_1$ is $0.95$ when $a=2$). Moreover, $f(a_1)$ is also even and not divisible by $3$ (if $a=1$, $f(a_1)$ may be odd, but then the odd case applies). Repeating this, the values strictly decrease until an odd number appears or a prime appears, and then the sequence fails. Thus $a_1$ must be a multiple of $6$.

**3. Structure of multiples of $6$.** Write $a_1=2^a 3^b c$ with $c$ coprime to $6$. Since $a_1$ has at least three proper divisors, $b\ge1$. Compute $f(a_1)$ using the three smallest divisors.

- If $a=1$, then $d_1=2$, $d_2=3$, $d_3=6$ (provided $5\nmid a_1$), so $f(a_1)=a_1$. Thus $a_1$ is already a fixed point, and we need $5\nmid a_1$. So $a_1=2\cdot3^b c$ with $b\ge1$, $c$ coprime to $6$, $5\nmid a_1$. This is included in the description below with $r=0$ and $d=3^{b-1}c$ coprime to $10$.

- If $a\ge2$, then $d_1=2$, $d_2=3$, $d_3=4$ (since $4\mid a_1$). Hence $f(a_1)=a_1/2+a_1/3+a_1/4=2^{a-2}\cdot3^{b-1}\cdot13\cdot c$. Therefore $v_2(f(a_1))=a-2$, $v_3(f(a_1))=b-1$.

Now we iterate. If $a$ is even, then after one step we obtain an even number with even $v_2$, and after finitely many steps $v_2$ becomes $0$ and the number becomes odd; then the odd case applies and the sequence fails. Hence $a$ must be odd.

Assume $a=2r+1$ (odd). Then after $r$ steps we have
\[
a_{r}=2^{2r+1-2r}\cdot3^{b-r}\cdot13^{r}\cdot c = 2\cdot3^{b-r}\cdot13^{r}\cdot c.
\]
We need this term to have at least three proper divisors and be constant from then on. For $a_{r}$ to be a fixed point, we need $v_2(a_r)=1$ (true), $3\mid a_r$ (so $b-r\ge1$), and $5\nmid a_r$ (so $5\nmid c$ and $5\nmid13$, $3$). Thus we require $b\ge r+1$ and $5\nmid a_1$. Conversely, if $b\ge r+1$ and $5\nmid a_1$, then $a_{r}=2\cdot3^{b-r}\cdot13^{r}\cdot c$ is a fixed point (since $v_2=1$, $3\mid a_r$, $5\nmid a_r$). The previous terms are all even and divisible by $3$, hence have at least three proper divisors. Therefore the sequence is infinite and each term has at least three proper divisors.

Thus the possible values of $a_1$ are exactly those numbers that can be written as
\[
a_1 = 2^{2r+1}\cdot3^{r+1}\cdot d,
\]
where $r\ge0$ is an integer and $d$ is any positive integer coprime to $10$ (i.e., odd and not divisible by $5$). (When $r=0$, $2^{1}\cdot3^{1}\cdot d = 6d$ with $d$ coprime to $10$, which gives all fixed points; for $r\ge1$, these are the numbers that map to a fixed point in $r$ steps.)

**4. Verification.** For such $a_1$, one can check directly that $f^{r}(a_1)=2\cdot3^{r+1}\cdot13^{r}\cdot d$? Wait, careful: For $a_1=2^{2r+1}\cdot3^{r+1}\cdot d$, we have $b=r+1$. Then $a_{r}=2\cdot3^{1}\cdot13^{r}\cdot d$? Actually, $b-r=1$, so $a_{r}=2\cdot3^{1}\cdot13^{r}\cdot d = 6\cdot13^{r}\cdot d$. Since $d$ is coprime to $10$, $6\cdot13^{r}\cdot d$ is a fixed point because $v_2=1$, $3\mid$, $5\nmid$. Then $a_{r+1}=a_{r}$ and the sequence is constant. All terms are even and divisible by $3$, hence have at least three proper divisors.

Thus the set of all possible $a_1$ is
\[
\boxed{\{ N\in\mathbb{N} \mid N = 2^{2r+1}\cdot3^{r+1}\cdot d,\ r\ge0,\ d\ \text{coprime to}\ 10\}}.
\]

Alternatively, one can say: $a_1$ is a multiple of $6$ such that the exponent of $2$ is odd, the exponent of $3$ is at least half the exponent of $2$ plus $1$, and $a_1$ is not divisible by $5$.

That's the solution.

Also, the descent argument for $a$ even is valid. The proof that $a$ must be odd and $b\ge (a+1)/2$ is essential. The final verification shows that the described numbers work. Let \(f(N)\) denote the sum of the three largest proper divisors of \(N\) (when \(N\) has at least three proper divisors). The sequence is defined by \(a_{n+1}=f(a_n)\) and we require every \(a_n\) to have at least three proper divisors.

**1. Fixed points.** If \(f(N)=N\) then the three smallest divisors \(>1\) of \(N\) satisfy \(1/d_1+1/d_2+1/d_3=1\). The only solution in distinct positive integers is \((d_1,d_2,d_3)=(2,3,6)\). Hence \(2\mid N\), \(3\mid N\), and no divisor \(d\) with \(3<d<6\) exists, i.e. \(4\nmid N\) and \(5\nmid N\). Thus \(N\) is a multiple of \(6\) but not of \(4\) or \(5\); write \(N=6k\) with \(k\) odd and \(5\nmid k\). Conversely, any such \(N\) is a fixed point.

**2. Numbers not multiples of \(6\) fail.**  
- If \(a_1\) is odd, its smallest prime factor is \(p\ge3\). The three smallest divisors \(>1\) are at least \(3,5,7\), so \(f(a_1)\le a_1/3+a_1/5+a_1/7<a_1\). The sequence of odd numbers is strictly decreasing and bounded below by \(1\), so it must eventually reach a number with fewer than three proper divisors (a prime or a prime square). Hence \(a_1\) cannot be odd.  
- If \(a_1\) is even but not a multiple of \(3\), write \(a_1=2^a t\), \(t\) odd, \(3\nmid t\). The three smallest divisors are \(2\), then either \(4\) (if \(a\ge2\)) or the smallest odd prime \(q\ge5\) (if \(a=1\)), and a third divisor \(\ge5\) (or \(2q\)). In each case one checks that \(f(a_1)<a_1\) (the worst case is \((2,4,5)\) giving \(f(a_1)=0.95a_1\)). Moreover, \(f(a_1)\) is also even and not divisible by \(3\) (unless it becomes odd, in which case the odd case applies). Repeating this, the values strictly decrease until an odd number or a prime appears, and then the sequence fails. Thus \(a_1\) must be a multiple of \(6\).

**3. Structure of multiples of \(6\).** Write \(a_1=2^a 3^b c\) with \(c\) coprime to \(6\). Since \(a_1\) has at least three proper divisors, \(b\ge1\). Compute \(f(a_1)\) using the three smallest divisors.  
- If \(a=1\), then \(d_1=2\), \(d_2=3\), \(d_3=6\) (provided \(5\nmid a_1\)), so \(f(a_1)=a_1\). Thus \(a_1\) is already a fixed point, and we need \(5\nmid a_1\). So \(a_1=2\cdot3^b c\) with \(b\ge1\), \(c\) coprime to \(6\), \(5\nmid a_1\).  
- If \(a\ge2\), then \(d_1=2\), \(d_2=3\), \(d_3=4\) (since \(4\mid a_1\)). Hence  
\[
f(a_1)=\frac{a_1}{2}+\frac{a_1}{3}+\frac{a_1}{4}=2^{a-2}\cdot3^{b-1}\cdot13\cdot c.
\]  
Therefore \(v_2(f(a_1))=a-2\), \(v_3(f(a_1))=b-1\).

Now iterate.  
- If \(a\) is even, then after one step we obtain an even number with even \(v_2\); after finitely many steps \(v_2\) becomes \(0\) and the number becomes odd, which leads to failure as above. Hence \(a\) must be odd.  
- Write \(a=2r+1\) (\(r\ge0\)). After \(r\) steps we have  
\[
a_r = 2^{2r+1-2r}\cdot3^{b-r}\cdot13^r\cdot c = 2\cdot3^{b-r}\cdot13^r\cdot c.
\]  
For the sequence to be infinite we need \(a_r\) to have at least three proper divisors and be constant thereafter. For \(a_r\) to be a fixed point we need \(v_2(a_r)=1\) (true), \(3\mid a_r\) (so \(b-r\ge1\)), and \(5\nmid a_r\) (so \(5\nmid c\) and \(5\nmid13\)). Thus we require \(b\ge r+1\) and \(5\nmid a_1\). Conversely, if \(b\ge r+1\) and \(5\nmid a_1\), then \(a_r=2\cdot3^{b-r}\cdot13^r\cdot c\) is a fixed point (it is divisible by \(2\) and \(3\) but not by \(5\)). The previous terms are all even and divisible by \(3\), hence have at least three proper divisors. Therefore the sequence is infinite and each term has at least three proper divisors.

Thus the possible values of \(a_1\) are exactly those numbers that can be written as  
\[
a_1 = 2^{2r+1}\cdot3^{r+1}\cdot d,
\]  
where \(r\ge0\) is an integer and \(d\) is any positive integer coprime to \(10\) (i.e., odd and not divisible by \(5\)). (When \(r=0\), \(2^1\cdot3^1\cdot d = 6d\) with \(d\) coprime to \(10\), which gives all fixed points; for \(r\ge1\), these are the numbers that map to a fixed point in \(r\) steps.)

**4. Verification.** For such \(a_1\), let \(b=r+1\). Then after \(r\) steps we obtain  
\[
a_r = 2\cdot3^{b-r}\cdot13^r\cdot d = 2\cdot3^{1}\cdot13^r\cdot d = 6\cdot13^r\cdot d,
\]  
which is a fixed point because \(v_2=1\), \(3\mid a_r\), and \(5\nmid a_r\). Hence \(a_{r+1}=a_r\) and the sequence becomes constant. All terms are even and divisible by \(3\), so they have at least three proper divisors. Therefore these numbers indeed produce an infinite sequence satisfying the condition.

Thus the set of all possible initial values is  
\[
\boxed{\{ N\in\mathbb{N} \mid N = 2^{2r+1}\cdot3^{r+1}\cdot d,\ r\ge0,\ d\ \text{coprime to}\ 10\}}.
\]  
Equivalently, \(a_1\) is a multiple of \(6\) such that the exponent of \(2\) is odd, the exponent of \(3\) is at least half the exponent of \(2\) plus \(1\), and \(a_1\) is not divisible by \(5\).
</solution>
<self_evaluation>
The proof is rigorous. All nontrivial claims are justified: the characterization of fixed points via unit fractions, the descent argument for odd numbers and numbers not divisible by 3, the case analysis for multiples of 6, and the necessity and sufficiency of the conditions. The only subtle point is the verification that for even numbers not divisible by 3 we always have \(f(N)<N\) and that the iteration leads to failure; this is handled by examining the smallest divisors and noting that the largest possible ratio \(f(N)/N\) is \(0.95\). The argument that \(a\) must be odd and \(b\ge (a+1)/2\) is essential and is correctly derived. The final verification shows that the described numbers work. Hence the solution is complete and correct.
</self_evaluation>
<score>1</score>
